<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41588"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Gossypium barbadense and Gossypium hirsutum genomes provide insights into the origin and evolution of allotetraploid cotton"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Genetics 2019 51:4"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2019-03-18"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2019 The Author(s), under exclusive licence to Springer Nature America, Inc."/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Allotetraploid cotton is an economically important natural-fiber-producing crop worldwide. After polyploidization, Gossypium hirsutum L. evolved to produce a higher fiber yield and to better survive harsh environments than Gossypium barbadense, which produces superior-quality fibers. The global genetic and molecular bases for these interspecies divergences were unknown. Here we report high-quality de novo&#8211;assembled genomes for these two cultivated allotetraploid species with pronounced improvement in repetitive-DNA-enriched centromeric regions. Whole-genome comparative analyses revealed that species-specific alterations in gene expression, structural variations and expanded gene families were responsible for speciation and the evolutionary history of these species. These findings help to elucidate the evolution of cotton genomes and their domestication history. The information generated not only should enable breeders to improve fiber quality and resilience to ever-changing environmental conditions but also can be translated to other crops for better understanding of their domestication history and use in improvement. High-quality de novo&#8211;assembled genomes of two cultivated allotetraploid cotton species and whole-genome comparative analyses provide insights into the evolution of cotton genomes and improvement of fiber quality and resilience to stress."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Allotetraploid cotton is an economically important natural-fiber-producing crop worldwide. After polyploidization, <i>Gossypium hirsutum</i> L. evolved to produce a higher fiber yield and to better survive harsh environments than <i>Gossypium barbadense</i>, which produces superior-quality fibers. The global genetic and molecular bases for these interspecies divergences were unknown. Here we report high-quality de novoâassembled genomes for these two cultivated allotetraploid species with pronounced improvement in repetitive-DNA-enriched centromeric regions. Whole-genome comparative analyses revealed that species-specific alterations in gene expression, structural variations and expanded gene families were responsible for speciation and the evolutionary history of these species. These findings help to elucidate the evolution of cotton genomes and their domestication history. The information generated not only should enable breeders to improve fiber quality and resilience to ever-changing environmental conditions but also can be translated to other crops for better understanding of their domestication history and use in improvement.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Main"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Main</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Each crop has its own unique origin and diversification and domestication histories that have altered its genetic composition, resulting in a variety of physiological and phenotypic differences in comparison to wild relatives<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Doebley, J. F., Gaut, B. S. &amp; Smith, B. D. The molecular genetics of crop domestication. Cell 127, 1309â1321 (2006)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d10098e910">1</a></sup>. The genus <i>Gossypium</i> is an attractive model for studying the origin, evolution and domestication of polyploids. It contains two cultivated tetraploids, <i>G. hirsutum</i> L. (AD<sub>1</sub>) and <i>G. barbadense</i> L. (AD<sub>2</sub>), which originated in the New World from transoceanic hybridization of an A-genome-like ancestral African species, <i>Gossypium herbaceum</i> or <i>Gossypium arboreum</i>, with a native D-genome-like species, <i>Gossypium raimondii</i> (D<sub>5</sub>), ~1â1.5 million years ago (MYA)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Endrizzi, J., Turcotte, E. &amp; Kohel, J. Genetics, cytogenetics and evolution of Gossypium. Adv. Genet. 23, 271â375 (1985)." href="#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d10098e939">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Wendel, J. F. New World tetraploid cottons contain Old World cytoplasm. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 86, 4132â4136 (1989)." href="#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d10098e939_1">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Zhang, T. et al. Sequencing of allotetraploid cotton (Gossypium hirsutum L. acc. TM-1) provides a resource for fiber improvement. Nat. Biotechnol. 33, 531â537 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d10098e942">4</a></sup>. The nascent allotetraploid diverged into five cotton species ~1 MYA (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Liu, X. et al. Gossypium barbadense genome sequence provides insight into the evolution of extra-long staple fiber and specialized metabolites. Sci. Rep. 5, 14139 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d10098e947">5</a></sup>). Of these, <i>G. hirsutum</i> and <i>G. barbadense</i> independently evolved and were domesticated in diverse geographic regions. Domesticated <i>G. hirsutum</i> has shown broader adaptability due to its high yield potential with moderate fiber quality and accounts for ~90% of annual world cotton output, whereas <i>G. barbadense</i> produces exceptionally high-quality fibers and confers a competitive advantage to specialty cotton textiles producers.</p><p>The global genetic and molecular bases underlying the evolutionary dynamics associated with the origin, speciation and diversification of these two tetraploid species are not well understood. The cultivated tetraploid cottons<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Zhang, T. et al. Sequencing of allotetraploid cotton (Gossypium hirsutum L. acc. TM-1) provides a resource for fiber improvement. Nat. Biotechnol. 33, 531â537 (2015)." href="#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d10098e966">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Liu, X. et al. Gossypium barbadense genome sequence provides insight into the evolution of extra-long staple fiber and specialized metabolites. Sci. Rep. 5, 14139 (2015)." href="#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d10098e966_1">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Li, F. et al. Genome sequence of cultivated Upland cotton (Gossypium hirsutum TM-1) provides insights into genome evolution. Nat. Biotechnol. 33, 524â530 (2015)." href="#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d10098e966_2">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Yuan, D. et al. The genome sequence of Sea-Island cotton (Gossypium barbadense) provides insights into the allopolyploidization and development of superior spinnable fibres. Sci. Rep. 5, 17662 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d10098e969">7</a></sup> and their corresponding diploid progenitors<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Du, X. et al. Resequencing of 243 diploid cotton accessions based on an updated A genome identifies the genetic basis of key agronomic traits. Nat. Genet. 50, 796â802 (2018)." href="#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d10098e973">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Li, F. et al. Genome sequence of the cultivated cotton Gossypium arboreum. Nat. Genet. 46, 567â572 (2014)." href="#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d10098e973_1">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Paterson, A. H. et al. Repeated polyploidization of Gossypium genomes and the evolution of spinnable cotton fibres. Nature 492, 423â427 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d10098e976">10</a></sup> have been sequenced. However, intergenic DNA such as telomeres, centromeres and repeat-rich regions are often poorly represented in the published draft genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Zhang, T. et al. Sequencing of allotetraploid cotton (Gossypium hirsutum L. acc. TM-1) provides a resource for fiber improvement. Nat. Biotechnol. 33, 531â537 (2015)." href="#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d10098e980">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Liu, X. et al. Gossypium barbadense genome sequence provides insight into the evolution of extra-long staple fiber and specialized metabolites. Sci. Rep. 5, 14139 (2015)." href="#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d10098e980_1">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Li, F. et al. Genome sequence of cultivated Upland cotton (Gossypium hirsutum TM-1) provides insights into genome evolution. Nat. Biotechnol. 33, 524â530 (2015)." href="#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d10098e980_2">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Yuan, D. et al. The genome sequence of Sea-Island cotton (Gossypium barbadense) provides insights into the allopolyploidization and development of superior spinnable fibres. Sci. Rep. 5, 17662 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d10098e983">7</a></sup>, leading to underestimation of many important genomic features found in these regions. Here we report two highly accurate, contiguous, chromosome-scale de novo assemblies of the <i>G. hirsutum</i> and <i>G. barbadense</i> genomes obtained by integrating non-PCR-based short-read sequencing, long-read-based gap closure, scaffolding, and orientation based on 3D proximity information derived from chromosome conformation capture (Hi-C) data and from optical and genetic maps. By using these two well-assembled genomes, we performed genome-wide comparative studies that unraveled the genomic components responsible for contrasting features between the species, demonstrating their origin in homeolog losses, genome restructuring in the postpolyploidization era and altered patterns of gene expression.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Assembly of high-quality genomes</h3><p>We adopted a hierarchical approach to perform chromosome-scale assembly (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">1</a>). For <i>G. barbadense</i> L. cv. Hai7124, about 800 Gb of high-quality sequence (&gt;330Ã genome coverage) generated from a series of PCR-free libraries, mate-paired libraries and a 10x Genomics library (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">1</a>) was assembled by using the DeNovoMAGIC3 software package (NRGene), which has been used extensively to assemble the complex wheat (<i>Triticum</i> spp.)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Avni, R. et al. Wild emmer genome architecture and diversity elucidate wheat evolution and domestication. Science 357, 93â97 (2017)." href="#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d10098e1017">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="International Wheat Genome Sequencing Consortium. Shifting the limits in wheat research and breeding using a fully annotated reference genome. Science 361, eaar7191 (2018)." href="#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d10098e1017_1">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Luo, M. C. et al. Genome sequence of the progenitor of the wheat D genome Aegilops tauschii. Nature 551, 498â502 (2017)." href="#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d10098e1017_2">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Zhao, G. Y. et al. The Aegilops tauschii genome reveals multiple impacts of transposonsNat. Plants 3, 946â955 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d10098e1020">14</a></sup>, opium<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Guo, L. et al. The opium poppy genome and morphinan production. Science 362, 343â347 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d10098e1025">15</a></sup> and maize<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Springer, N. M. et al. The maize W22 genome provides a foundation for functional genomics and transposon biology. Nat. Genet. 50, 1282â1288 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d10098e1029">16</a></sup> genomes. For Hai7124, we initially generated an assembly that captured 2.23 Gb of sequence (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">2</a>). The initial scaffolds were then corrected and merged by using optical map data (Bionano Genomics) (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">3</a>). Finally, we used 3D proximity information from Hi-C (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">4</a>) and our updated ultra-dense genetic map with 6,165,057 SNPs (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">5</a>) to check, order and orient the resulting superscaffolds. A total of 115 erroneously assembled loci were detected and corrected (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">6</a>). The final Hai7124 (v1.1) assembly captured 2.22 Gb of genome sequence, covering approximately 91.4% of the total genome size, which was estimated to be 2.43 Gb by <i>k</i>-mer analysis (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">2</a>). The contiguity of the newly assembled genome was 47- and 90-fold greater than that of the two published draft genomes of <i>G. barbadense</i> (scaffold N50â=â23.44 Mb versus 0.50 Mb (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Liu, X. et al. Gossypium barbadense genome sequence provides insight into the evolution of extra-long staple fiber and specialized metabolites. Sci. Rep. 5, 14139 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d10098e1058">5</a></sup>) and 0.26 Mb (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Yuan, D. et al. The genome sequence of Sea-Island cotton (Gossypium barbadense) provides insights into the allopolyploidization and development of superior spinnable fibres. Sci. Rep. 5, 17662 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d10098e1062">7</a></sup>)). Approximately 98.2% of the 2.22-Gb assembled genome was assigned to 26 chromosomes (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#Tab1">1</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">7</a>). The gap size (Ns) was substantially reduced to 32.46 Mb in Hai7124 (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">3</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Summary of the final genome assemblies for TM-1 and Hai7124</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We also generated an improved de novoâassembled genome for <i>G. hirsutum</i> L. acc. TM-1 (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">8</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">9</a>). The total assembly size was 2.30 Gb, with the longest scaffold 41.92 Mb in length. About 97.4% of the 2.30-Gb assembled genome was oriented and organized into 26 chromosomes (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#Tab1">1</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">10</a>). In comparison with two recently published genome assemblies for this accession, the updated TM-1 reference assembly (v2.1) showed higher contiguity, that is, ~10- to 20-fold longer scaffold N50 values (15.5 Mb versus 1.6 Mb (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Zhang, T. et al. Sequencing of allotetraploid cotton (Gossypium hirsutum L. acc. TM-1) provides a resource for fiber improvement. Nat. Biotechnol. 33, 531â537 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d10098e1360">4</a></sup>) and 0.764 Mb (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Li, F. et al. Genome sequence of cultivated Upland cotton (Gossypium hirsutum TM-1) provides insights into genome evolution. Nat. Biotechnol. 33, 524â530 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d10098e1364">6</a></sup>)). The total size of the oriented assembled genome without connective N sequences was dramatically increased (2,211 Mb for TM-1 (v2.1) versus 1,667 Mb for TM-1 (v1.1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Zhang, T. et al. Sequencing of allotetraploid cotton (Gossypium hirsutum L. acc. TM-1) provides a resource for fiber improvement. Nat. Biotechnol. 33, 531â537 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d10098e1368">4</a></sup>) (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">4</a>). We validated the contiguity and accuracy of the new assembly by using the same 36 BAC sequences (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">5</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">11</a>) and integrated genetic and cytogenetic maps of the A12 and D12 homeologous chromosomes from FISH (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">6</a>) as we reported previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Zhang, T. et al. Sequencing of allotetraploid cotton (Gossypium hirsutum L. acc. TM-1) provides a resource for fiber improvement. Nat. Biotechnol. 33, 531â537 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d10098e1385">4</a></sup>.</p><p>We successfully assembled and filled the gaps in the new genomes, and erroneous assemblies, especially those spanning centromeric regions, were corrected (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#Fig1">1a,b</a> and Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">7</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">8</a>). The alignment between Hai7124 (v1.1) and <i>G.barbadense</i> accession 3-79 was largely incomplete (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#Fig1">1c</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">8</a>), probably owing to the fragmented nature of the 3-79 assembly (scaffold N50â=â260 kb, scaffold numberâ=â29,751)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Yuan, D. et al. The genome sequence of Sea-Island cotton (Gossypium barbadense) provides insights into the allopolyploidization and development of superior spinnable fibres. Sci. Rep. 5, 17662 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d10098e1411">7</a></sup>. The highly consistent alignment of the genome sequences to the corresponding optical maps (88% mapping rate in TM-1 and 91% in Hai7124) (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#Fig1">1a,b</a> and Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">7</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">8</a>) further validates the high contiguity and accuracy of our new genome assemblies.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Syntenic characterization of the TM-1 and Hai7124 genomes."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1: Syntenic characterization of the TM-1 and Hai7124 genomes.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-019-0371-5/MediaObjects/41588_2019_371_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-019-0371-5/MediaObjects/41588_2019_371_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="762" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Collinearity on chromosome A01 for the TM-1 (v1.1) and TM-1 (v2.1) genomes. The red region in the blue bar indicates the centromeric region identified by alignment of CRGs. Gray lines indicate collinearity between the two genomes; each line represents a unique mapping alignment &gt;5 kb in length. Yellow bars show the CenH3-binding regions in each genome; adjusted ChIP-seq reads were calculated for a 100-kb sliding window at 10-kb intervals. GC content, repeat distribution and gene density in a 1-Mb sliding window are shown at 100-kb intervals. A comparison of TM-1 Bionano optical maps (blue) and the TM-1 (v2.1) genome (green) is shown. Right, TM-1 Hi-C contact data mapped onto the TM-1 (v2.1) genome. The heat map represents the normalized contact matrix. <b>b</b>, Collinearity on chromosome A01 for the Hai7124 (v1.1) and 3-79 genomes. Data are presented as in <b>a</b>. <b>c</b>, Analysis of the synteny among multiple <i>Gossypium</i> genomes. Macrosynteny connecting blocks of &gt;30 one-to-one gene pairs is shown. Blue links indicate translocation between the D subgenome of allotetraploid cotton and the D genome of diploid cotton; yellow links indicate translocation between the A genome and the D genome in diploid cotton; and green links indicate translocation between the A subgenome of allotetraploid cotton and the A genome of diploid cotton. <b>d</b>, MCScanX was performed to identify the collinearity between genes in the TM-1 (v2.1) and Hai7124 (v1.1) genomes. Each block includes &gt;30 gene pairs.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Notable improvement in the assembly of centromeres</h3><p>Fine-scale genetic and physical mapping of centromeres in multiple crop species remains a mammoth task. In total, 955, 1,012, 205, 284 and 100 CRG (centromere retroelement <i>Gossypium</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Luo, S. et al. The cotton centromere contains a Ty3-gypsy-like LTR retroelement. PLoS One 7, e35261 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d10098e1472">17</a></sup> homologs were identified in the centromeric region of each chromosome for TM-1 (v2.1), Hai7124 (v1.1), TM-1 (v1.1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Zhang, T. et al. Sequencing of allotetraploid cotton (Gossypium hirsutum L. acc. TM-1) provides a resource for fiber improvement. Nat. Biotechnol. 33, 531â537 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d10098e1476">4</a></sup>, Xinhai 21 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Liu, X. et al. Gossypium barbadense genome sequence provides insight into the evolution of extra-long staple fiber and specialized metabolites. Sci. Rep. 5, 14139 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d10098e1480">5</a></sup>) and 3-79 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Yuan, D. et al. The genome sequence of Sea-Island cotton (Gossypium barbadense) provides insights into the allopolyploidization and development of superior spinnable fibres. Sci. Rep. 5, 17662 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d10098e1484">7</a></sup>), respectively, by using our previously described approach<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Zhang, T. et al. Sequencing of allotetraploid cotton (Gossypium hirsutum L. acc. TM-1) provides a resource for fiber improvement. Nat. Biotechnol. 33, 531â537 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d10098e1489">4</a></sup>. The average length of the centromeric region predicted for each chromosome with a 95% confidence interval around the median was 270 kb in Hai7124 (v1.1) and 358 kb in TM-1 (v2.1), which is substantially less than the 9.23 Mb in TM-1 (v1.1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Zhang, T. et al. Sequencing of allotetraploid cotton (Gossypium hirsutum L. acc. TM-1) provides a resource for fiber improvement. Nat. Biotechnol. 33, 531â537 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d10098e1493">4</a></sup>, the 6.80 Mb in Xinhai 21 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Liu, X. et al. Gossypium barbadense genome sequence provides insight into the evolution of extra-long staple fiber and specialized metabolites. Sci. Rep. 5, 14139 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d10098e1497">5</a></sup>) and the 11.81 Mb in 3-79 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Yuan, D. et al. The genome sequence of Sea-Island cotton (Gossypium barbadense) provides insights into the allopolyploidization and development of superior spinnable fibres. Sci. Rep. 5, 17662 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d10098e1501">7</a></sup>) (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">9</a> and Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">12</a>â<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">16</a>).</p><p>The centromeric DNA regions associated with centromere-specific H3 (CenH3)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Su, H. et al. Dynamic location changes of Bub1-phosphorylated-H2AThr133 with CENH3 nucleosome in maize centromeric regions. New Phytol. 214, 682â694 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d10098e1518">18</a></sup> were further identified by ChIP-seq using an antibody to cotton CenH3. In total, 66.72 and 50.16 million reads (151 bp) were obtained from the TM-1 and Hai7124 ChIP-seq libraries, respectively. In FISH experiments, distinct and high-intensity signals were observed in the centromeric region of each chromosome, further confirming the CenH3 ChIP DNA enrichment (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">10</a>). Approximately 22.10 and 17.04 million sequence reads were mapped to unique sites in the genomes for TM-1 (v2.1) and Hai7124 (v1.1), respectively. As a result, potential centromeric regions from all 26 chromosomes of the Hai7124 (v1.1) assembly and 24 chromosomes of the TM-1 (v2.1) assembly (with the exception of D02 and D08) were identified (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#Fig1">1a,b</a>, Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">7</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">8</a>, and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">17</a>). The CenH3 ChIP-seq data mapped to a sharp interval on each chromosome, ranging from 0.60 to 2.85 Mb in length, which was highly overlapped by the region identified by the CRGs (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">12</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">13</a>). However, the CenH3 ChIP-seq reads could not be mapped onto the TM-1 (v1.1) assembly (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#Fig1">1a</a> and Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">7</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">8</a>), likely owing to the fragmented nature of its centromeric regions. Moreover, centromeres showed consistent localization along the same chromosome in the two cotton species (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">17</a>), implying a high level of genome conservation and a relatively short time to divergence. In plants, extensive studies have been conducted to isolate and map the DNA corresponding to CenH3-associated centromeric chromatin by using ChIP-seq<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Jiang, J. &amp; Birchler, J. A. Plant Centromere Biology (Wiley-Blackwell, 2013)." href="#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d10098e1557">19</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Schneider, K. L., Xie, Z., Wolfgruber, T. K. &amp; Presting, G. G. Inbreeding drives maize centromere evolution. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 113, 987â996 (2016)." href="#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d10098e1557_1">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Wang, K., Wu, Y., Zhang, W., Dawe, R. K. &amp; Jiang, J. Maize centromeres expand and adopt a uniform size in the genetic background of oat. Genome Res. 24, 107â116 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d10098e1560">21</a></sup>. However, poorly assembled reference centromeres often hamper the deployment of ChIP-seq, which has resulted in poor characterization of the centromeres of the sequenced plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Gong, Z. et al. Repeatless and repeat-based centromeres in potato: implications for centromere evolution. Plant Cell 24, 3559â3574 (2012)." href="#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d10098e1564">22</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Han, J. et al. Rapid proliferation and nucleolar organizer targeting centromeric retrotransposons in cotton. Plant J. 88, 992â1005 (2016)." href="#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d10098e1564_1">23</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Zhu, Z. et al. The NnCenH3 protein and centromeric DNA sequence profiles of Nelumbo nucifera Gaertn (sacred lotus) reveal the DNA structures and dynamics of centromeres in basal eudicots. Plant J. 87, 568â582 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d10098e1567">24</a></sup>. Until now, only five centromeres from <i>Brachypodium distachyon</i>, a small-genome grass, had been fully sequenced and assembled, which was possible because these centromeres have a relatively low percentage of highly repetitive DNA (~21.4%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="International Brachypodium Initiative. Genome sequencing and analysis of the model grass Brachypodium distachyon. Nature 463, 763â768 (2010)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d10098e1574">25</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Li, Y. et al. Centromeric DNA characterization in the model grass Brachypodium distachyon provides insights on the evolution of the genus. Plant J. 93, 1088â1101 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d10098e1577">26</a></sup>. These results demonstrate that our new genome assemblies have made substantial improvement in sequence continuity in almost all regions, including repetitive-DNA-rich centromeric regions.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Gene prediction and annotation</h3><p>A total of 72,761 and 75,071 high-confidence protein-coding genes (PCGs) were predicted for TM-1 (v2.1) and Hai7124 (v1.1), respectively, of which &gt;96% were supported by full-length transcript data (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">18</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">19</a>). For most of these genes (95%; <i>n</i>â=â100), there was complete accuracy between the identified exonâintron boundaries and those determined by using mapped RNA-seq reads (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">11</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">20</a>). Benchmarking Universal Single-Copy Orthologs (BUSCO) analysis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Simao, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210â3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d10098e1605">27</a></sup> revealed full gene content for 97.5% of PCGs in TM-1 and for 97.3% of PCGs in Hai7124 (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#Tab1">1</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">21</a>); of these genes, the majority were retained in duplicate, largely owing to the allotetraploid nature of the genomes. In total, 5,558 and 5,606 genes in TM-1 and Hai7124, respectively, were putative transcription factors belonging to 58 families, representing 7.6% and 7.5% of the PCGs (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">22</a>).</p><p>A total of 1,460.46 Mb and 1,374.61 Mb of transposable element (TE) sequence accounted for 62.2â63.9% of the total genome size for TM-1 and Hai7124, respectively (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">23</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">24</a>). The TE content in the A subgenome (979.05 Mb in TM-1 and 922.04 Mb in Hai7124) was twice that identified in the D subgenome (443.23 Mb in TM-1 and 420.66 Mb in Hai7124) (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">23</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">24</a>). The majority of TEs were <i>Gypsy</i> long terminal repeat (LTR) retroelements, including 41.4% of TEs in <i>G. hirsutum</i> and 39.5% of TEs in <i>G. barbadense</i>. The distribution of the divergence rate for each type of TE was similar for the two genomes (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">12</a>). Furthermore, we predicted the insertion time of LTRs by examining the sequence divergence at both ends of intact LTRs between TM-1 and Hai7124. Two expansions were identified that were distinct from the amplification wave of complete LTR elements (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">13</a>). These two recent LTR insertion events seem to have occurred around 2 MYA and 13 MYA, close to the time of tetraploid cotton formation (1â2 MYA)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Zhang, T. et al. Sequencing of allotetraploid cotton (Gossypium hirsutum L. acc. TM-1) provides a resource for fiber improvement. Nat. Biotechnol. 33, 531â537 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d10098e1649">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Paterson, A. H. et al. Repeated polyploidization of Gossypium genomes and the evolution of spinnable cotton fibres. Nature 492, 423â427 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d10098e1652">10</a></sup> and the divergence time between diploid A- and D-genome species (2â13 MYA)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Li, F. et al. Genome sequence of cultivated Upland cotton (Gossypium hirsutum TM-1) provides insights into genome evolution. Nat. Biotechnol. 33, 524â530 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d10098e1656">6</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Speciation and genome evolution</h3><p>We resequenced 17 accessions representing the D genome and wild AD genome (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">25</a>), finding that <i>G. raimondii</i> (D<sub>5</sub>) was the closest relative to the D subgenome for all five allotetraploids (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">14</a>). Furthermore, whole-genome sequence alignment of our current reference-quality assemblies to genomes for the diploid progenitors <i>G. arboreum</i> (A genome)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Du, X. et al. Resequencing of 243 diploid cotton accessions based on an updated A genome identifies the genetic basis of key agronomic traits. Nat. Genet. 50, 796â802 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d10098e1683">8</a></sup> and <i>G. raimondii</i> (D genome)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Paterson, A. H. et al. Repeated polyploidization of Gossypium genomes and the evolution of spinnable cotton fibres. Nature 492, 423â427 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d10098e1690">10</a></sup> demonstrated that the two tetraploids have highly collinear relationships with their diploid progenitors. However, some reciprocal translocations were observed along chromosomes 1â3, as well as along chromosomes 4 and 5 (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#Fig1">1c</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">26</a>), in accordance with earlier reports<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Wendel, J. F. &amp; Cronn, R. C. Polyploidy and the evolutionary history of cotton. Adv. Agron. 78, 139â186 (2003)." href="#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d10098e1700">28</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Menzel, M. Y. &amp; Brown, M. S. The significance of multivalent formation in three-species Gossypium hybrids. Genetics 39, 546â557 (1954)." href="#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d10098e1700_1">29</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Gerstel, D. U. Chromosomal translocations in interspecific hybrids of the genus Gossypium. Evolution 7, 11 (1953)." href="#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d10098e1700_2">30</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Brubaker, C. L., Paterson, A. H. &amp; Wendel, J. F. Comparative genetic mapping of allotetraploid cotton and its diploid progenitors. Genome 42, 184â203 (1999)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d10098e1703">31</a></sup>. A total of 2.2 Gb of sequence from Hai7124 (v1.1) could be mapped onto the TM-1 (v2.1) genome, suggesting that the collinearity and gene order for these species are largely conserved (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#Fig1">1d</a>). These data support the idea that <i>G. hirsutum</i> and <i>G. barbadense</i> originated from a common allotetraploid ancestor.</p><p>The nonsynonymous (<i>K</i><sub>a</sub>) and synonymous (<i>K</i><sub>s</sub>) substitution rates for 22,054 orthologous gene sets were compared between <i>G. arboreum</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Du, X. et al. Resequencing of 243 diploid cotton accessions based on an updated A genome identifies the genetic basis of key agronomic traits. Nat. Genet. 50, 796â802 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d10098e1730">8</a></sup>, <i>G. raimondii</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Paterson, A. H. et al. Repeated polyploidization of Gossypium genomes and the evolution of spinnable cotton fibres. Nature 492, 423â427 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d10098e1737">10</a></sup>, and the A and D subgenomes of the tetraploid species. On the basis of this, the divergence time for the A and D progenitor genomes was estimated to be around 6.2â7.1 MYA, and the allotetraploid formed around 1.7â1.9 MYA (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#Fig2">2a</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">27</a>). The divergence of <i>G. barbadense</i> and <i>G. hirsutum</i> occurred ~0.4â0.6 MYA (<i>K</i><sub>s</sub> peaks at 0.002 and 0.003, respectively).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Evolution of the allopolyploid cotton genome."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2: Evolution of the allopolyploid cotton genome.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-019-0371-5/MediaObjects/41588_2019_371_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-019-0371-5/MediaObjects/41588_2019_371_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="740" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Evolutionary analysis of the <i>G. barbadense</i> and <i>G. hirsutum</i> genomes. The <i>K</i><sub>s</sub> distribution is shown for orthologs in the cotton genomes; data are grouped into <i>K</i><sub>s</sub> units of 0.001. Species are represented by their initials; At, A subgenome; Dt, D subgenome. <b>b</b>, Comparison of the codon substitution rate distribution between the two subgenomes of TM-1 and Hai7124 and their progenitors. The central line for each box plot indicates the median, the top and bottom edges indicate the seventy-fifth and twenty-fifth percentiles, and the whiskers extend to 1.5 times the interquartile range. Asterisks indicate significant differences (Wilcoxon rank-sum test, <i>P</i>â&lt;â0.05). <b>c</b>, Distribution of SNPs, indels, PAVs, inversions and translocations across the TM-1 genome in comparison to the Hai7124 genome. Regions of &gt;500 kb that showed lower than expected SNP frequencies are indicated by green blocks along the bottom track, and those with higher than expected SNP frequencies are indicated by red blocks.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We identified 9,451 one-to-one orthologous gene sets among the genome assemblies for <i>Theobroma cacao</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Argout, X. et al. The genome of Theobroma cacao. Nat. Genet. 43, 101â108 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d10098e1809">32</a></sup>, the A and D diploid progenitors, and the A and D subgenomes of our two currently assembled cultivated species, which suggests an accelerated evolution rate in allotetraploids in comparison to their diploid progenitors. A faster evolution rate was observed in allotetraploid cottons in comparison to their living diploid progenitors; in the A subgenome in comparison to the D subgenome; and in <i>G. barbadense</i> in comparison to <i>G. hirsutum</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#Fig2">2b</a>, Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">15</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">28</a>). In addition, greater numbers of lost, disrupted and positively selected genes were found in the A subgenome (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">16</a>), indicating that the A subgenome might have experienced strong selection pressures during evolution.</p><p>Deep sequencing data (average coverage of 60Ã) from nine <i>G. barbadense</i> and ten <i>G. hirsutum</i> accessions (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">29</a>) showed high divergence between <i>G. hirsutum</i> and <i>G. barbadense</i>, as well as between the wild <i>G.</i> <i>hirsutum</i> race yucatanense and the domesticated and/or improved accessions for <i>G. hirsutum</i> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">17a</a>). Furthermore, a long reciprocally introgressed region exhibiting extremely low levels of polymorphism, extending from 43.10 Mb to 92.00 Mb on chromosome A01, was found by comparing the <i>G. hirsutum</i> race with <i>G. barbadense</i> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">17b</a>), supporting the idea that hybridization occurred between domesticated species and their wild ancestors in the spread of domestication and/or diversification, as we reported previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Fang, L. et al. Genomic insights into divergence and dual domestication of cultivated allotetraploid cottons. Genome Biol. 18, 33 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d10098e1873">33</a></sup>. In addition, Xinhai 25 showed introgression from the <i>G. hirsutum</i> race at 48.70â57.90 Mb and 81.60â92.00 Mb. Junhai 1 showed introgression from the <i>G. hirsutum</i> race at 48.40â54.50 Mb and 75.00â92.00 Mb (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">17b</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">30</a>). The introgressed region from the long-lived, perennial <i>G. hirsutum</i> race on chromosome A01 was found in nine <i>G. barbadense</i> accessions collected from Egyptian, American Pima and Central Asian ELS (extra-long-staple) cottons, but not in the Tanguis landrace, which produces coarse fibers with medium staple length, which is typical of current cottons from Peru. It was confirmed that <i>G. barbadense</i> originated in a region spanning northwestern Peru and southwestern Ecuador<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Westengen, O. T., Huaman, Z. &amp; Heun, M. Genetic diversity and geographic pattern in early South American cotton domestication. Theor. Appl. Genet. 110, 392â402 (2005)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d10098e1899">34</a></sup>. Introgression of wild and/or domesticated alleles from <i>G. hirsutum</i> may have induced changes in âmorpho-physiological traitsâ, helping in the domestication of Sea Island cotton (<i>G. barbadense</i>). This analysis of introgressed regions further revealed that there were two genotypes among the Central Asian ELS cottons: one was the same as the Egyptian or American Pima type, represented by Hai7124 and Xinhai 21, and the other is a new type, represented by Junhai 1 and Xinhai 25, selected from cultivar 9122 from the former Soviet Union. Therefore, the spread and adaptation of domesticated cottons to different agro-ecological and cultural environments have led to phenotypic and genetic divergence among domesticated populations to produce two cultivated allotetraploid cotton types.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Postpolyploidization genome restructuring</h3><p>Genetically, <i>G. hirsutum</i> and <i>G. barbadense</i> are cross-compatible, but their interspecific hybrids exhibit genetic breakdown during segregation, which needs to be investigated. Alignment of Hai7124 to the TM-1 genome identified 13,845,619 SNPs and 1,358,773 indels (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">31</a>), with 5.98 SNPs and 0.59 indels per kilobase on average. We also detected 34 SNP-poor and 5 SNP-rich regions, covering 90.4 Mb and 6.2 Mb of the genome, respectively (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#Fig2">2c</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">32</a>). Of these variations, 200,528â213,634 SNPs and 7,926â8,453 indels were found in coding regions. A total of 10,366 genes with sequence variations (GSVs) resulting in gain or loss of stop codons and frameshifts, among other changes, were identified (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM3">33</a>). These GSVs were classified into three types: type I consisted of 3,029 GSVs identified in Hai7124 but annotated only in TM-1 and type III consisted of 3,556 GSVs identified in TM-1 but annotated only in Hai7124. The 3,781 type II GSVs were annotated in both species (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#Fig3">3a</a>). 69.7% of the species-specific GSVs were completely validated in the 19 deep-sequenced accessions (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM3">34</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM3">35</a>). Gene Ontology (GO) enrichment analysis showed that the type I GSVs were enriched for genes involved in defense response and DNA integration, the type II GSVs were enriched for genes involved in response to stress and oxidationâreduction processes, and the type III GSVs were enriched for genes involved in DNA integration and cell recognition. The variations mapping to these GSVs may be among the causative mutations that have led to the phenotypic divergence between these two species. For instance, among the type II GSVs, a 2-bp (CA) deletion in <i>WLIM1a</i> was identified in Hai7124 (<i>GB_D06G0243</i>) that resulted in premature termination of translation. Thus, in comparison to the wild-type homolog in TM-1 (<i>GH_D06G0228</i>), there were 23 amino acids deleted from the encoded protein in Hai7124, including 5 in the second LIN-11, Isl1 and MEC-3 (LIM) motif (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">18a</a>). This species-specific indel was further confirmed in all 19 accessions (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">18b</a>). The protein encoded by <i>WLIM1a</i> has a role in cotton fiber elongation as well as secondary cell wall (SCW) formation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Han, L. B. et al. The dual functions of WLIM1a in cell elongation and secondary wall formation in developing cotton fibers. Plant Cell 25, 4421â4438 (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d10098e1965">35</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Structural variations between TM-1 and Hai7124."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3: Structural variations between TM-1 and Hai7124.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-019-0371-5/MediaObjects/41588_2019_371_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-019-0371-5/MediaObjects/41588_2019_371_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="377" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Classification of GSVs identified through comparison of TM-1 and Hai7124. Type I and type III GSVs correspond to genes annotated in only TM-1 and Hai7124, respectively. Type II GSVs correspond to genes containing sequence variations between TM-1 and Hai7124. The <i>y</i> axis shows the length ratio of GSVs between TM-1 and Hai7124. <b>b</b>, Length and distribution of TE insertions and deletions identified through comparison of TM-1 and Hai7124. <b>c</b>, TE annotation in the peaks within the regions at 3 kb, 4.9 kb and 11 kb highlighted in <b>b</b>.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>By comparing TM-1 and Hai7124, we further identified 10,377 presence regions (101 bp to 99,272 bp) and 12,903 absence regions (101 bp to 96,795 bp) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#Fig3">3b</a>), 10 inversions (1,111 bp to 44,679 bp) and 32 translocations (2,530 bp to 11,439 bp) (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">36</a>). Among these, 1,358 structural variations were in genes (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM3">37</a>), and 70.1% of these were consistent with the classification of the 19 deep-sequenced accessions (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM3">38</a>). The presence/absence variants (PAVs) were distributed unevenly across the genomes; the majority of these were <i>Copia</i> elements (LTR type) found in coding DNA sequence (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#Sec38">19a,b</a>). The expression level of genes with LTR insertions was lower than that of genes in which PAVs occurred in upstream or downstream sequences or in introns and that of all PCGs expressed in root, stem, leaf or fiber (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">19c</a>), but these genes showed much more differential expression between <i>G. hirsutum</i> and <i>G. barbadense</i> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">19d</a>). Of the three major PAV types, the first (74%; ~3 kb) and second (94%; ~11 kb) types were caused by <i>Gypsy</i> LTR retroelements, whereas the third type (68%; ~4.9 kb) was mostly caused by <i>Copia</i> LTR retroelements (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#Fig3">3c</a>). These structural variations were found to be associated with loss of function of 820 and 791 genes in TM-1 and Hai7124, respectively (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">39</a>). In <i>G. barbadense</i>, we detected a PAV in an Î±-expansin gene with fiber-specific expression (<i>GB_A10G1730</i>; deletion of 450 bp within exon 3), which resulted in a truncated protein lacking the C-terminal polysaccharide-binding domain as compared with the <i>G. hirsutum</i> homolog (<i>GH_A10G1626</i>, also known as <i>GhEXP1</i>). The protein encoded by this truncated ELS-fiber-related gene can significantly enhance fiber length, fineness and strength through cell wall restructuring in <i>G. hirsutum</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Li, Y. et al. GbEXPATR, a species-specific expansin, enhances cotton fibre elongation through cell wall restructuring. Plant Biotechnol. J. 14, 951â963 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d10098e2068">36</a></sup>. This species-specific deletion in the expansin gene (<i>GB_A10G1730</i> versus <i>GH_A10G1626</i>) was found in nine of the <i>G. barbadense</i> accessions examined. Furthermore, within the SCW-biosynthesis-related cellulose synthase CESA 7 clade (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">20a</a>), a large TE insertion (9,427 bp) in <i>GH_A07G0437</i> resulted in the absence of 53 amino acids from all ten <i>G. hirsutum</i> accessions but not the nine <i>G. barbadense</i> lines except for Pima S-4 (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">20b</a>). The corresponding wild-type CESA 7 gene (<i>GB_A07G0431</i>) showed high expression at 20 days post anthesis (DPA) during the SCW biosynthesis stage in all three <i>G. barbadense</i> accessions examined, suggesting that it potentially has a role in the biosynthesis of more cellulose to develop ELS fibers (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">20c</a>). These TE insertions might also have had a key role in cotton domestication and diversification.</p><p>Moreover, we identified 3,905 copy number variations (CNVs) of sequences that were either gained or lost in the 19 cotton accessions in comparison to TM-1 (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">40</a>). The genes within CNVs were significantly enriched for genes involved in defense response (<i>P</i>â=â2.60âÃâ10<sup>â12</sup>, Fisherâs exact test), suggesting that they have a role in adaptation to various environments.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Genomic insights into development of extra-long fibers in <i>G. barbadense</i>
                        </h3><p>We measured dynamic changes in the elongating fibers of TM-1 and Hai7124 plants from 5 to 40 DPA and observed a nearly linear increase in fiber length at 5â15 DPA in TM-1 plants, whereas this increase occurred at 5â20 DPA in Hai7124 plants (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#Fig4">4a</a>). The maximum elongation rate was achieved at 15â20 DPA; this rate suddenly decreased at 25 DPA in TM-1 fibers but was retained until 30 DPA in Hai7124 fibers (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#Fig4">4b</a>). The extended elongation phase in Hai7124 plants (5â30 DPA) most likely leads to development of longer fibers in <i>G. barbadense</i> as compared to TM-1 plants (elongation from 5â25 DPA).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Model showing the molecular mechanism responsible for development of longer fibers in G. barbadense."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4: Model showing the molecular mechanism responsible for development of longer fibers in <i>G. barbadense</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-019-0371-5/MediaObjects/41588_2019_371_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-019-0371-5/MediaObjects/41588_2019_371_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="730" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Phenotypes of fiber-bearing seeds in Hai7124 and TM-1 plants. Numbers above the graphs are DPA. Scale bar, 10 mm. <b>b</b>, Fiber elongation patterns for TM-1 and Hai7124. Each value is the meanâÂ±âs.d. of fiber length for at least ten seeds from three individual plants at a given time point. <b>c</b>â<b>e</b>, Comparison of the content of the main osmotically active solutes soluble sugar (<b>c</b>), malate (<b>d</b>) and K<sup>+</sup> (<b>e</b>) between TM-1 and Hai7124 fibers. Mean values and s.e.m. were calculated from three biological replicates for soluble sugar and from six biological replicates for K<sup>+</sup> and malate. DW, dry weight. <b>f</b>, Transmission electron microscopy (TEM) analysis of epidermal cell structure in 5-DPA ovule, showing the large central vacuole in the fiber cell compared with fiber base and epidermal cell.Scale bars, 10 Âµm in the figure and 100 nm in the inset. PD, plasmodesmata; C, cytosol; V, vacuole; CW, cell wall. <b>g</b>, Model depicting the roles of open PD and the genes related to the main osmotically active solutes in fiber elongation. Suc, sucrose; M, malate; Fru, fructose; Glu, glucose. <b>h</b>â<b>k</b>, qRTâPCR analysis of <i>TST1</i> (<b>h</b>), <i>VIN1</i> (<b>i</b>), <i>ALMT16</i> (<b>j</b>) and <i>NHX1</i> (<b>k</b>) in fibers from TM-1 plants at 5â25 DPA and from Hai7124 plants at 5â30 DPA. Three biological replicates were assessed per reaction, each with two technical replicates (using the same sample). Each value represents the meanâÂ±âs.e.m. **<i>P</i>â&lt;â0.01, Studentâs <i>t</i> test. The expression of <i>TST1</i>, <i>VIN1</i>, <i>NHX1</i> and <i>ALMT16</i> in fibers from TM-1 plants at 30 DPA is not shown because RNA could not be extracted from fibers at this time point.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Fiber elongation is driven by cell turgor created by the influx of sucrose and potassium ions (K<sup>+</sup>) and local production of malate together with high expression of expansion-related genes encoding proteins that loosen the cell wall matrix<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Andres, Z. et al. Control of vacuolar dynamics and regulation of stomatal aperture by tonoplast potassium uptake. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 111, E1806âE1814 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d10098e2250">37</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Ruan, Y. L., Llewellyn, D. J. &amp; Furbank, R. T. The control of single-celled cotton fiber elongation by developmentally reversible gating of plasmodesmata and coordinated expression of sucrose and K+ transporters and expansin. Plant Cell 13, 47â60 (2001)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d10098e2253">38</a></sup>. The content of the osmotically active solutes in elongating fibers was substantially increased in Hai7124 plants from 10 to 15 DPA, and this high level was sustained from 15 DPA onward (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#Fig4">4câe</a>). A large central vacuole occupied 98% of the volume of quickly elongating fiber cells (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#Fig4">4f</a>); such vacuoles store the main osmotically active solutes that impose maximal turgor pressure for fiber elongation .</p><p>To the extent of our knowledge, the underlying molecular basis of ELS fiber production has not previously been investigated. Here we found 45,129 and 45,328 genes expressed (FPKMâ&gt;â1) in developing single-celled fibers of TM-1 and Hai7124 plants, respectively. Kyoto Encyclopedia of Genes and Genomes (KEGG) annotation of these genes revealed that genes associated with membrane transport, transcription, and glycan biosynthesis and carbon metabolism were significantly enriched for expression in Hai7124 as compared to TM-1 fibers (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">21a</a>), highlighting the species-specific role of these genes in fiber elongation.
