Furthermore, the sucrose transporter (<i>GbTST1</i>), Na<sup>+</sup>/H<sup>+</sup> antiporter (<i>GbNHX1</i>) and aluminum-activated malate transporter (<i>GbALMT16</i>) genes whose expression was detected in the tonoplast of Hai7124 fibers had a longer period of expression than the corresponding genes in TM-1 (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#Fig4">4gâk</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">41</a>). These transporters pump more sucrose, K<sup>+</sup> and malate into the vacuole<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Barragan, V. et al. Ion exchangers NHX1 and NHX2 mediate active potassium uptake into vacuoles to regulate cell turgor and stomatal function in Arabidopsis. Plant Cell 24, 1127â1142 (2012)." href="#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d10098e2291">39</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Bassil, E. et al. The Arabidopsis Na+/H+ antiporters NHX1 and NHX2 control vacuolar pH and K+ homeostasis to regulate growth, flower development, and reproduction. Plant Cell 23, 3482â3497 (2011)." href="#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d10098e2291_1">40</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Hedrich, R., Sauer, N. &amp; Neuhaus, H. E. Sugar transport across the plant vacuolar membrane: nature and regulation of carrier proteins. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 25, 63â70 (2015)." href="#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d10098e2291_2">41</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Meyer, S., De Angeli, A., Fernie, A. R. &amp; Martinoia, E. Intra- and extra-cellular excretion of carboxylates. Trends Plant Sci. 15, 40â47 (2010)." href="#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d10098e2291_3">42</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Meyer, S. et al. Malate transport by the vacuolar AtALMT6 channel in guard cells is subject to multiple regulation. Plant J. 67, 247â257 (2011)." href="#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d10098e2291_4">43</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Nei, M. &amp; Kumar, S. Molecular evolution and phylogenetics. Heredity 86, 385â385 (2000)." href="#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d10098e2291_5">44</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Wang, L. &amp; Ruan, Y. L. Unraveling mechanisms of cell expansion linking solute transport, metabolism, plasmodesmtal gating and cell wall dynamics. Plant Signal. Behav. 5, 1561â1564 (2010)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d10098e2294">45</a></sup>. Moreover, sucrose is further degraded into hexose by the vacuole-localized vacuolar invertase (<i>VIN1</i>) to enhance the osmotic potential required for fiber elongation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Wang, L., Cook, A., Patrick, J. W., Chen, X. Y. &amp; Ruan, Y. L. Silencing the vacuolar invertase gene GhVIN1 blocks cotton fiber initiation from the ovule epidermis, probably by suppressing a cohort of regulatory genes via sugar signaling. Plant J. 78, 686â696 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d10098e2302">46</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Wang, L. et al. Evidence that high activity of vacuolar invertase is required for cotton fiber and Arabidopsis root elongation through osmotic dependent and independent pathways, respectively. Plant Physiol. 154, 744â756 (2010)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d10098e2305">47</a></sup>. Interestingly, <i>GbVIN1</i> also showed prolonged expression in Hai7124 as compared to TM-1 fibers (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#Fig4">4i</a>).</p><p>Unlike malate, which is synthesized in the cytosol of fibers, sucrose and K<sup>+</sup> enter fiber cells through the plasma membrane or plasmodesmata (PD)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Wang, L. et al. Evidence that high activity of vacuolar invertase is required for cotton fiber and Arabidopsis root elongation through osmotic dependent and independent pathways, respectively. Plant Physiol. 154, 744â756 (2010)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d10098e2320">47</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Li, X. R., Wang, L. &amp; Ruan, Y. L. Developmental and molecular physiological evidence for the role of phosphoenolpyruvate carboxylase in rapid cotton fibre elongation. J. Exp. Bot. 61, 287â295 (2010)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d10098e2323">48</a></sup>. In Hai7124 plants, the PD remained open for a longer time (5â15 DPA) to import sucrose into the cytosol of developing fiber cells from underlying seed coat cells than it did in TM-1 plants (5â10 DPA) (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">21b</a>). Conversely, expression of the plasma membraneâlocalized SUTs and SWEETs responsible for sucrose transport is almost completely silenced during this stage<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Zhang, Z. et al. Suppressing a putative sterol carrier gene reduces plasmodesmal permeability and activates sucrose transporter genes during cotton fiber elongation. Plant Cell 29, 2027â2046 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d10098e2330">49</a></sup>. All these findings demonstrate that expression of <i>GbVIN1</i>, <i>GbTST1</i>, <i>GbNHX1</i> and <i>GbALMT16</i> and PD opening for a relatively extended time period could be responsible for the accumulation of more soluble sugar, K<sup>+</sup> and malate, thereby leading to production of longer fibers in ELS cotton.</p><p>In total, there were 1,464 genes representing expansions in 446 gene families (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM3">42</a>) in the Hai7124 genome, significantly more than the 696 genes found in the TM-1 genome (<i>P</i>â=â6.27âÃâ10<sup>â60</sup>, Fisherâs exact test) (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#Sec38">43</a>). For instance, the 12 genes in class I of the ADP-ribosylation factor (ARF) GTPase family, forming core components of vesicle transport machinery and determining epidermal cell polarity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Naramoto, S. et al. ADP-ribosylation factor machinery mediates endocytosis in plant cells. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 107, 21890â21895 (2010)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d10098e2364">50</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Xu, J. &amp; Scheres, B. Dissection of Arabidopsis ADP-RIBOSYLATION FACTOR 1 function in epidermal cell polarity. Plant Cell 17, 525â536 (2005)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d10098e2367">51</a></sup>, correspond to a specific expansion in Hai7124 (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">22a</a>). These ARF GTPase genes were predominantly expressed during fiber elongation (10 DPA) and SCW thickening (20 and 25 DPA), indicating that they potentially have a role in the development of longer and stronger fibers in ELS cotton (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">22b</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Divergent evolution of genes involved in abiotic stress tolerance</h3><p>No significant differences in response to salinity or drought stresses were observed between TM-1 and Hai7124 (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#Sec38">23a,b</a>). However, TM-1 seedlings were more tolerant to heat and cold stresses than Hai7124 seedlings (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#Fig5">5aâd</a>), suggesting that selection after domestication was aimed at developing the high yield and adaptation abilities of <i>G. hirsutum</i>. We detected 16,029 versus 5,270 genes conferring resistance to heat stress (<i>P</i>â=â0, Fisherâs exact test) and 8,725 versus 12,475 genes conferring resistance to cold stress (<i>P</i>â=â5.079âÃâ10<sup>â142</sup>, Fisherâs exact test) in TM-1 and Hai7124, respectively (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">44</a>). In total, 51 genes including <i>PIPK</i>, <i>PIP</i> (plasma membraneâintrinsic protein), <i>CaM</i> genes and downstream transcriptional regulator (<i>HSF</i>) genes involved in the ability of plants to sense heat<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Mittler, R., Finka, A. &amp; Goloubinoff, P. How do plants feel the heat? Trends Biochem. Sci. 37, 118â125 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d10098e2420">52</a></sup> showed differential responses to heat stress between TM-1 and Hai7124 (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">41</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">45</a>). Most of these (39 genes), especially the <i>CaM</i> genes, were specifically up- or downregulated under heat stress in <i>G. hirsutum</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#Fig5">5b</a>). The ethylene signaling pathway was also activated under heat stress in TM-1, as nine each of the genes encoding ethylene receptors and ethylene response factors (ERFs) were dramatically upregulated in TM-1 (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#Fig5">5b,c</a>, Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">23c</a> and Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">41</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">45</a>), elucidating their role in regulating <i>EIN3</i> and <i>EIL1</i> activation under heat stress<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Kendrick, M. D. &amp; Chang, C. Ethylene signaling: new levels of complexity and regulation. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 11, 479â485 (2008)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d10098e2459">53</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Models depicting the molecular basis of heat and cold tolerance in TM-1."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5: Models depicting the molecular basis of heat and cold tolerance in TM-1.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-019-0371-5/MediaObjects/41588_2019_371_Fig5_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-019-0371-5/MediaObjects/41588_2019_371_Fig5_HTML.png" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="728" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p><b>a</b>,<b>d</b>, Phenotypic comparison of TM-1 and Hai7124 cotton seedlings in response to heat (<b>a</b>) and cold (<b>d</b>) stress. Photographs were taken under normal conditions or for seedlings subjected to heat treatment at 37âÂ°C or cold treatment at 4 Â°C for 120 h followed by recovery for 24 h. Scale bars, 10 cm. <b>b</b>,<b>e</b>, Heat maps showing differentially expressed genes involved in the ethylene signaling pathway for heat stress (<b>b</b>) and the ABA signaling pathway for cold stress (<b>e</b>) in TM-1 and Hai7124. Red, upregulation; yellow, downregulation; blue, no difference in expression; white, absence of an orthologous gene. <b>c</b>, A proposed model showing that the ethylene and plasma membrane signaling pathways cooperatively enhance heat tolerance in <i>G. hirsutum</i>. These pathways work together to maintain high transcriptional activities under heat stress, whereas no difference in expression is observed in <i>G. barbadense</i>. <b>f</b>, Diagram of the cold-responsive transcriptional network in cotton. The ABA and calcium signaling pathways were activated in <i>G. hirsutum</i>, whereas only a small portion of the differentially expressed genes were activated in <i>G. barbadense</i>. Upward- and downward-pointing arrows indicate up- and downregulated genes; red and blue numbers correspond to the number of differently expressed genes in TM-1 and Hai7124, respectively.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>TM-1 seedlings also showed greater tolerance of cold stress than Hai7124 seedlings (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#Fig5">5d</a>). In total, 23 genes from the abscisic acid (ABA) signaling pathway that encode ABA receptors (PYR/PYL), the SnRK protein kinase and the PP2C protein phosphatase kinase (ABI/AHG/PP2C)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Raghavendra, A. S., Gonugunta, V. K., Christmann, A. &amp; Grill, E. ABA perception and signalling. Trends Plant Sci. 15, 395â401 (2010)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d10098e2532">54</a></sup> showed differential responses to cold in TM-1 and Hai7124 (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#Fig5">5e,f</a>). Most genes, especially those encoding the ABA receptors ABAR (CHLH), PYR1, PYL3, PYL8 and PYL11, were specifically upregulated under cold stress in TM-1 (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">46</a>), and this upregulation was confirmed by qRTâPCR analysis (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">23d</a>). These genes have in part facilitated the adaptation of Upland cotton to new environments.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec10" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec10-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec10">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec10-content"><p>Here we performed de novo assembly of the genomes of two cultivated allotetraploid cotton species by integrating data from Illumina PCR-free short-read sequencing, 10x Genomics sequencing, Hi-C, and optical and super-dense genetic maps. These efforts resulted in substantial improvements to the contiguity and accuracy of assembly, with a notable improvement in the assembly of centromeres. By analyzing the two resulting high-quality genome assemblies, we determined that tissue- and/or developmental-stage-specific expression and divergent neo- and subfunctionalization of homeologs between <i>G. hirsutum</i> and <i>G. barbadense</i> may have led to the variable adaptation responses of these two species after the formation of allotetraploid cottons. Cotton evolutionary processes are also partially driven by the cultural and economic importance of cotton to humans. To our knowledge, this is the first comparison of these two important allotetraploid cotton species over the entire genome. These results should improve understanding of crop evolution, domestication and diversification and should lead to the discovery of novel domestication-related genes conferring agronomically beneficial traits in future breeding programs and evolutionary trajectories in plants.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec11" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec11-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec11">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec11-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">DNA extraction and sequencing</h3><p>Genomic DNA from <i>G. hirsutum</i> L. acc. TM-1 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Kohel, R., Richmond, T. &amp; Lewis, C. Texas Marker-1. Description of a genetic standard for Gossypium hirsutum L. Crop Sci. 10, 670â671 (1970)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d10098e2575">55</a></sup>) (genetic standard) and <i>G. barbadense</i> L. cv. Hai7124 (resistant to verticillium wilt) seedlings was isolated by a modified CTAB method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Paterson, A. H., Brubaker, C. L. &amp; Wendel, J. F. A rapid method for extraction of cotton (Gossypium spp.) genomic DNA suitable for RFLP or PCR analysis. Plant Mol. Biol. Rep. 11, 122â127 (1993)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d10098e2582">56</a></sup>. Five size-selected DNA libraries (470 bp to 10 kb) and three mate-pair libraries (2â5, 5â7 and 7â10 kb) were constructed. To limit sequencing biases across genomic regions with varying GC content, libraries with insert sizes of ~470 and 800 bp were constructed by using TruSeq DNA Sample Preparation Kit version 2 with no PCR amplification. The library with an insert size of ~470 bp was sequenced on a HiSeq 2500 v2 instrument in rapid mode as 2âÃâ265 bp reads, which produced âstitchedâ reads of ~265â520 bp in length. The 800-bp shotgun library and the mate-pair libraries were sequenced on a HiSeq 4000 instrument as 2âÃâ150 bp reads. Construction and sequencing of paired-end and mate-pair libraries were carried out at the Roy J. Carver Biotechnology Center at the University of Illinois at Urbana-Champaign.</p><p>DNA molecules longer than 50 kb prepared with the nuclei method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Zhang, M. et al. Preparation of megabase-sized DNA from a variety of organisms using the nuclei method for advanced genomics research. Nat. Protoc. 7, 467â478 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d10098e2589">57</a></sup> were used to construct a 10x Genomics library that was sequenced on the GemCode platform (10x Genomics, Pleasanton) at the Hudson Alpha Institute for Biotechnology. About 800 Gb of sequencing data was produced for both TM-1 and Hai7124, respectively (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">1</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Initial genome assembly by DeNovoMAGIC3</h3><p>A flowchart of contig, scaffold and chromosome assembly in this study is shown in Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">1</a>. Initial genome assembly was performed by deploying the DeNovoMAGIC3 software platform (NRGene), a de Bruijn graphâbased assembler. This task was accomplished by using accurate read-based traveling in the graph that iteratively connected consecutive contigs over local repeats to generate long scaffolds. An additional long-range barcoded DNA library (Chromium system by 10x Genomics) was sequenced to support haplotyping of heterozygous genomes, scaffold validation and further longer-range extension of the scaffolds. The algorithm has been described in detail in wheat D-genome assembly<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Avni, R. et al. Wild emmer genome architecture and diversity elucidate wheat evolution and domestication. Science 357, 93â97 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d10098e2607">11</a></sup>. de Bruijn graphs (<i>k</i>-merâ=â127 bp) of contigs were built from all paired-end and mate-pair reads. Next, paired-end reads were used to find reliable paths in the graph between contigs to resolve repeats and extend contigs.</p><p>The barcoded reads from the 10x Genomics library were mapped to the assembled scaffolds, and clusters of reads with the same barcode mapped to adjacent contigs in the scaffolds were identified as being part of a single long molecule. Each scaffold was scanned with a 20-kb window to ensure that the number of distinct clusters that covered the entire window (indicating support for this 20-kb connection by several long molecules) was statistically significant with respect to the number of clusters that spanned the left and right edges of the window. If a potential scaffold assembly error was detected, the scaffold was broken at the two edges of the suspicious 20-kb window. Finally, the barcodes mapping to the scaffold edges were compared (first and last 20-kb sequences) to generate a scaffold graph with a link connecting two scaffolds with more than two shared barcodes. Linear scaffold paths in the scaffold graph were composed into the final scaffold output of assembly. Initial genome assemblies are summarized in Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">2</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">8</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Integrating Bionano optical maps with the initial assembly</h3><p>High-molecular-weight DNA was extracted from yellowish cotyledons with a Bionano Plant Tissue DNA isolation kit (Bionano Genomics) and digested with Nt.BssSI (New England Biolabs). After labeling and staining, DNA was loaded onto the Saphyr chip for sequencing. 457 and 449 Gb of data (&gt;150 kb) for TM-1 and Hai7124, respectively, were collected and converted into a BNX file by AutoDetect software to obtain basic labeling and DNA length information. The filtered raw DNA molecules in BNX format were aligned, clustered and assembled into the BNG map by using the Bionano Genomics assembly pipeline. Alignment of sequence assemblies with the BNG map was computed with RefAligner, and visualization of alignment was performed with snapshot in IrysView. Combination of the genome maps with the initial assembly to produce a hybrid scaffold was performed sequentially with the two genome maps (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">3</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Construction of the updated POPSEQ genetic map</h3><p>We constructed an augmented POPSEQ genetic map by sequencing 241 F<sub>2</sub> individuals (approximately 5Ã genome coverage) derived from a cross between TM-1 and Hai7124, including 59 individuals used to construct the previous genetic map<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Wang, S. et al. Sequence-based ultra-dense genetic and physical maps reveal structural variations of allopolyploid cotton genomes. Genome Biol. 16, 108 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d10098e2644">58</a></sup>. After SNP calling and genotyping of the 241 F<sub>2</sub> individuals, simple SNPs from the parents and SNPs with the same genotype in the population were classified into a bin. Bins were assigned to linkage groups by JoinMap 3.0<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Van Ooijen, J. W. &amp; Voorrips, R. JoinMap: version 3.0 (Plant Research International, 2001)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d10098e2650">59</a></sup> with a minimum logarithm of odds (LOD) score of 13 and ordered by MSTmap<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Wu, Y., Bhat, P. R., Close, T. J. &amp; Lonardi, S. Efficient and accurate construction of genetic linkage maps from the minimum spanning tree of a graph. PLoS Genet. 4, e1000212 (2008)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d10098e2654">60</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Hi-C library construction and data collection</h3><p>We created three Hi-C libraries for TM-1 and Hai7124 by using the method described previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Xie, T. et al. De novo plant genome assembly based on chromatin interactions: a case study of Arabidopsis thaliana. Mol. Plant 8, 489â492 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d10098e2667">61</a></sup>. Libraries were subjected to sequencing on the Illumina HiSeq X Ten platform. Information about raw data is given in Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">4</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Ordering and orientation of the Bionano-assembled scaffolds by Hi-C and POPSEQ</h3><p>BNG maps were ordered and oriented on the basis of the POPSEQ genetic map. Hi-C data were then used to correct the Bionano assemblies. Hi-C reads were mapped to the assembled scaffolds with BWA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Fast and accurate short read alignment with BurrowsâWheeler transform. Bioinformatics 25, 1754â1760 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d10098e2682">62</a></sup> (version 0.7.15-r1140). PCR duplicates were removed with Novosort v1.04.06 (<a href="http://www.novocraft.com/">http://www.novocraft.com/</a>), and reads were excluded from subsequent analysis if they did not align within 500 bp of a restriction site or did not uniquely map. The initial ordered scaffolds were used to make the Hi-C map, and the scaffolds were then divided into bins of 500 kb in size and âlog<sub>10</sub> (number of Hi-C links) was calculated for all bins. The initial order and orientation were corrected and verified manually by using the Hi-C linking information; heat maps of the Hi-C data were plotted with HiCPlotter<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Akdemir, K. C. &amp; Chin, L. HiCPlotter integrates genomic data with interaction matrices. Genome Biol. 16, 198 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d10098e2695">63</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Assessment of TM-1 assembly using 36 completely sequenced BACs</h3><p>The 36 full-length BAC sequences<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Guo, W. et al. A preliminary analysis of genome structure and composition in Gossypium hirsutum. BMC Genomics 9, 314 (2008)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d10098e2707">64</a></sup> were split into 1-kb continuous windows and aligned to the TM-1 (v2.1) assembly by using BWA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Fast and accurate short read alignment with BurrowsâWheeler transform. Bioinformatics 25, 1754â1760 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d10098e2711">62</a></sup> (version 0.7.15-r1140) with the MEM algorithm. Windows mapping in the same chromosome region were used for graphical display.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">Identification of centromeric regions by CenH3 ChIP</h3><p>An antibody to CenH3 that showed specific binding to the cotton CenH3 protein was used in ChIP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Han, J. et al. Rapid proliferation and nucleolar organizer targeting centromeric retrotransposons in cotton. Plant J. 88, 992â1005 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d10098e2723">23</a></sup>. ChIP experiments were undertaken by following a published protocol<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Nagaki, K. et al. Sequencing of a rice centromere uncovers active genes. Nat. Genet. 36, 138â145 (2004)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d10098e2727">65</a></sup>. High-quality Illumina reads were mapped to our cotton genome assembly with Bowtie 2 (<a href="http://bowtie-bio.sourceforge.net/bowtie2/index.shtml">http://bowtie-bio.sourceforge.net/bowtie2/index.shtml</a>) as previously described<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Su, H. et al. Dynamic location changes of Bub1-phosphorylated-H2AThr133 with CENH3 nucleosome in maize centromeric regions. New Phytol. 214, 682â694 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d10098e2738">18</a></sup>. The genome was then divided into 10-kb non-overlapping windows and uniquely mapping reads (allowing 1-bp mismatch between each read and the reference genome) were counted. The read density was calculated by dividing the total number of uniquely mapping reads by the total number of mapped nucleotides in each genomic window. To remove the impact of non-specific binding by rabbit serum, the read density was adjusted for background signal by using mock control data. CenH3 domains were identified with SICER v1.1 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Zang, C. et al. A clustering approach for identification of enriched domains from histone modification ChIP-Seq data. Bioinformatics 25, 1952â1958 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d10098e2742">66</a></sup>). We used 200-bp windows, required fold change/control of â¥5 and false-discovery rate (FDR)â&lt;â0.01, and allowed gaps of 400 bp when defining the CenH3 domains.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20">RNA-seq data generation</h3><p>RNA was collected from the roots, stems, leaves and various reproductive organs of TM-1 and Hai7124 plants (details in Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM3">47</a>) and subjected to transcriptome sequencing, which yielded on average 25,530,023 reads for each replicate (three replicates for each sample) (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM3">47</a>). Illumina reads were aligned to the reference TM-1 genome (v2.1) using TopHat 2.1.1 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Kim, D. et al. TopHat2: accurate alignment of transcriptomes in the presence of insertions, deletions and gene fusions. Genome Biol. 14, R36 (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d10098e2760">67</a></sup>), and quantification of gene expression was performed with Cufflinks version 2.2.1 (<a href="http://cole-trapnell-lab.github.io/cufflinks/">http://cole-trapnell-lab.github.io/cufflinks/</a>) by using the GTF annotation file.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec21">PacBio sequencing and data analysis</h3><p>For TM-1 and Hai7124, total RNA was extracted from the roots, stems and leaves of 2-week-old plants; from whole mature flowers; from ovules and fibers at 0 and 5 DPA; from ovules at 10 and 20 DPA; and from fibers at 10 and 20 DPA. PacBio-seq library preparation was performed as previously described<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Wang, B. et al. Unveiling the complexity of the maize transcriptome by single-molecule long-read sequencing. Nat. Commun. 7, 11708 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d10098e2779">68</a></sup>. In total, data were generated from 33 SMRT cells. Sequence data were processed with SMRTlink 4.0 software. PacBio transcriptome reads (867,006 for TM-1 and 879,582 for Hai7124) were used in gene annotation.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec22">Gene prediction and annotation</h3><p>Gene prediction and annotation were conducted as previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Zhang, T. et al. Sequencing of allotetraploid cotton (Gossypium hirsutum L. acc. TM-1) provides a resource for fiber improvement. Nat. Biotechnol. 33, 531â537 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d10098e2792">4</a></sup>. Proteins from seven plant genomes (<i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>, <i>Carica papaya</i>, <i>Hibiscus syriacus</i>, <i>T. cacao</i>, <i>G. raimondii</i>, <i>G. arboreum</i> and <i>Vitis vinifera</i>) were downloaded from Phytozome (release 11)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Goodstein, D. M. et al. Phytozome: a comparative platform for green plant genomics. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, D1178âD1186 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d10098e2818">69</a></sup> and the corresponding species database<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Du, X. et al. Resequencing of 243 diploid cotton accessions based on an updated A genome identifies the genetic basis of key agronomic traits. Nat. Genet. 50, 796â802 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d10098e2822">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Paterson, A. H. et al. Repeated polyploidization of Gossypium genomes and the evolution of spinnable cotton fibres. Nature 492, 423â427 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d10098e2825">10</a></sup>. Protein sequences were aligned to the assembly using GenBlastA (version 1.0.4)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="She, R., Chu, J. S., Wang, K., Pei, J. &amp; Chen, N. GenBlastA: enabling BLAST to identify homologous gene sequences. Genome Res. 19, 143â149 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d10098e2829">70</a></sup>. GeneWise (version 2.4.1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Birney, E., Clamp, M. &amp; Durbin, R. GeneWise and Genomewise. Genome Res. 14, 988â995 (2004)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d10098e2834">71</a></sup> was used to predict the exact gene structure of the corresponding genomic regions for each GenBlastA hit. Five ab initio gene prediction programs, Augustus (version 3.2.1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Stanke, M., Schoffmann, O., Morgenstern, B. &amp; Waack, S. Gene prediction in eukaryotes with a generalized hidden Markov model that uses hints from external sources. BMC Bioinformatics 7, 62 (2006)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d10098e2838">72</a></sup>, Genscan (version 1.0)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Burge, C. &amp; Karlin, S. Prediction of complete gene structures in human genomic DNA. J. Mol. Biol. 268, 78â94 (1997)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d10098e2842">73</a></sup>, GlimmerHMM (version 3.0.4)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 74" title="Majoros, W. H., Pertea, M. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. TigrScan and GlimmerHMM: two open source ab initio eukaryotic gene-finders. Bioinformatics 20, 2878â2879 (2004)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d10098e2846">74</a></sup>, Geneid (version 1.4.4)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 75" title="Guigo, R. Assembling genes from predicted exons in linear time with dynamic programming. J. Comput. Biol. 5, 681â702 (1998)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR75" id="ref-link-section-d10098e2850">75</a></sup> and SNAP (version 2006-07-28)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 76" title="Korf, I. Gene finding in novel genomes. BMC Bioinformatics 5, 59 (2004)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d10098e2854">76</a></sup>, were used to predict coding regions in the repeat-masked genome. Finally, PacBio transcriptome reads were mapped to the assembly with BLAT (version 35)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 77" title="Kent, W. J. Blat: the BLAST-like alignment tool. Genome Res. 12, 656â664 (2002)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR77" id="ref-link-section-d10098e2859">77</a></sup> and RNA-seq data were mapped to the assembly with HISAT2 (version 2.0.1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 78" title="Kim, D., Langmead, B. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. HISAT: a fast spliced aligner with low memory requirements. Nat. Methods 12, 357â360 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR78" id="ref-link-section-d10098e2863">78</a></sup>; StringTie (version 1.2.2)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 79" title="Pertea, M. et al. StringTie enables improved reconstruction of a transcriptome from RNA-seq reads. Nat. Biotechnol. 33, 290â295 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR79" id="ref-link-section-d10098e2867">79</a></sup> and TransDecoder (v1.1.1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 80" title="Haas, B. J. et al. Automated eukaryotic gene structure annotation using EVidenceModeler and the Program to Assemble Spliced Alignments. Genome Biol. 9, R7 (2008)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR80" id="ref-link-section-d10098e2871">80</a></sup> were then used to assemble the transcripts and convert candidate coding regions into gene models. All gene models predicted with the above three approaches were combined by EvidenceModeler (EVM)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 80" title="Haas, B. J. et al. Automated eukaryotic gene structure annotation using EVidenceModeler and the Program to Assemble Spliced Alignments. Genome Biol. 9, R7 (2008)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR80" id="ref-link-section-d10098e2875">80</a></sup> into a non-redundant set of gene structures. The gene models were further filtered on the basis of their Cscore, protein coverage and coding sequence overlapping TEs, where Cscore is the BLASTP score ratio to the MBH (mutual best hit) BLASTP score and protein coverage is the highest percentage of the protein aligned to the best homolog. Only transcripts with Cscore â¥0.5 and protein coverage â¥0.5 were retained and the coding sequence overlapping repeats should be less than 20%; for gene models where more than 20% of the coding sequence overlapped repeats, the Cscore was required to be at least 0.9 and the protein coverage at least 70% for the model to be selected. Finally, gene models where more than 30% of the encoded protein was annotated as Pfam<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 81" title="Punta, M. et al. The Pfam protein families database. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, D290âD301 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR81" id="ref-link-section-d10098e2879">81</a></sup> or InterPro<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 82" title="Hunter, S. et al. InterPro in 2011: new developments in the family and domain prediction database. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, D306âD312 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR82" id="ref-link-section-d10098e2884">82</a></sup> TE domains were filtered out. Functional annotation of PCGs was achieved with BLASTP (version 2.2.26)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 83" title="Altschul, S. F. et al. Gapped BLAST and PSI-BLAST: a new generation of protein database search programs. Nucleic Acids Res. 25, 3389â3402 (1997)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR83" id="ref-link-section-d10098e2888">83</a></sup> (<i>E</i> value of 1âÃâ10<sup>â5</sup>) against two integrated protein sequence databases: SwissProt and TrEMBL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 84" title="Bairoch, A. &amp; Apweiler, R. The SWISS-PROT protein sequence data bank and its supplement TrEMBL in 1999. Nucleic Acids Res. 27, 49â54 (1999)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR84" id="ref-link-section-d10098e2897">84</a></sup>. Protein domains were annotated with InterProScan (v5.19)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 85" title="Jones, P. et al. InterProScan 5: genome-scale protein function classification. Bioinformatics 30, 1236â1240 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR85" id="ref-link-section-d10098e2901">85</a></sup>. The GO terms for each gene were retrieved with Blast2GO<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 86" title="Conesa, A. et al. Blast2GO: a universal tool for annotation, visualization and analysis in functional genomics research. Bioinformatics 21, 3674â3676 (2005)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR86" id="ref-link-section-d10098e2906">86</a></sup> based on the nr protein database. The pathways in which the genes might be involved were assigned by performing BLAST against the KEGG databases (release 59.3)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 87" title="Kanehisa, M. et al. Data, information, knowledge and principle: back to metabolism in KEGG. Nucleic Acids Res. 42, D199âD205 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR87" id="ref-link-section-d10098e2910">87</a></sup> (<i>E</i> value of 1âÃâ10<sup>â5</sup>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec23">Noncoding RNA annotation</h3><p>tRNA-encoding genes were predicted by tRNAscan-SE (version 1.3.1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 88" title="Lowe, T. M. &amp; Eddy, S. R. tRNAscan-SE: a program for improved detection of transfer RNA genes in genomic sequence. Nucleic Acids Res. 25, 955â964 (1997)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR88" id="ref-link-section-d10098e2927">88</a></sup>. rRNA fragments were predicted by aligning to <i>Arabidopsis</i> and rice template rRNA sequences by using BLASTN (version 2.2.26) with an <i>E</i>-value cutoff of 1âÃâ10<sup>â10</sup>. MicroRNA and small nuclear RNA (snRNA) genes were found by searching against the Rfam database (release 12.0)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 89" title="Griffiths-Jones, S. et al. Rfam: annotating non-coding RNAs in complete genomes. Nucleic Acids Res. 33, D121âD124 (2005)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR89" id="ref-link-section-d10098e2939">89</a></sup> with Infernal (version 1.1.1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 90" title="Nawrocki, E. P., Kolbe, D. L. &amp; Eddy, S. R. Infernal 1.0: inference of RNA alignments. Bioinformatics 25, 1335â1337 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR90" id="ref-link-section-d10098e2944">90</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec24">Repeat annotation</h3><p>Repeats in the assembled genomes were probed with RepeatMasker (version open-4.0.6; <a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org/">http://www.repeatmasker.org/</a>) run against the MIPS repeat database (mipsREdat_9.3p)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 91" title="Nussbaumer, T. et al. MIPS PlantsDB: a database framework for comparative plant genome research. Nucleic Acids Res. 41, D1144âD1151 (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR91" id="ref-link-section-d10098e2963">91</a></sup>. RepeatMasker results were filtered to retain high-confidence hits (lengthââ¥â50 bp, scoreââ¥â255). To estimate the insertion times for LTRs, we applied LTR_FINDER (v1.06)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 92" title="Xu, Z. &amp; Wang, H. LTR_FINDER: an efficient tool for the prediction of full-length LTR retrotransposons. Nucleic Acids Res. 35, W265âW268 (2007)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR92" id="ref-link-section-d10098e2967">92</a></sup> to identify LTRs in the cotton genome with parameters â-D 15000 -d 1000 -L 7000 -l 100 -p 20 -C -M 0.9â and then integrated the results and removed false positives from the initial predictions by the LTR_retriever pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 93" title="Ou, S. &amp; Jiang, N. LTR_retriever: a highly accurate and sensitive program for identification of long terminal repeat retrotransposons. Plant Physiol. 176, 01310 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR93" id="ref-link-section-d10098e2971">93</a></sup>; insertion time was estimated as <i>T</i>â=â<i>K</i>/2<i>r</i> (where <i>K</i> is the divergence rate and <i>r</i> is the neutral mutation rate; <i>r</i>â=â2.6âÃâ10<sup>â9</sup>) in the LTR_retriever package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 93" title="Ou, S. &amp; Jiang, N. LTR_retriever: a highly accurate and sensitive program for identification of long terminal repeat retrotransposons. Plant Physiol. 176, 01310 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR93" id="ref-link-section-d10098e2997">93</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec25">Identification of homeologous and orthologous gene sets</h3><p>A total of 9,282 one-to-one orthologous gene sets were identified for the <i>G. arboreum</i>, <i>G. raimondii</i> and <i>T. cacao</i> genomes and the two subgenomes of <i>G. hirsutum</i> and <i>G. barbadense</i> by using the OrthoMCL (version 1.4) clustering program<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 94" title="Li, L., Stoeckert, C. J. Jr. &amp; Roos, D. S. OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res. 13, 2178â2189 (2003)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR94" id="ref-link-section-d10098e3025">94</a></sup>. 21,419 one-to-one orthologous gene sets were identified for <i>G. raimondii</i>, <i>G. arboreum</i>, and the two subgenomes of <i>G. hirsutum</i> and <i>G. barbadense</i> by using BLASTP (version 2.2.26)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 83" title="Altschul, S. F. et al. Gapped BLAST and PSI-BLAST: a new generation of protein database search programs. Nucleic Acids Res. 25, 3389â3402 (1997)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR83" id="ref-link-section-d10098e3041">83</a></sup> based on the bidirectional best hit (BBH) method with sequence coverage &gt;30% and identity &gt;30%, followed by selection of the best match.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec26">Assessing the reference genome by BUSCO gene set</h3><p>Quality control on the integrity of the assembly was performed by using the independent BUSCO v2 benchmark (<a href="http://busco.ezlab.org/">http://busco.ezlab.org/</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Simao, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210â3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d10098e3060">27</a></sup>, which is used to specifically assess the integrity of genic regions.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec27">Phylogenetic tree construction and evolutionary rate estimation</h3><p>A phylogenetic tree was constructed for the seven genomes and subgenomes (<i>T. cacao</i>, <i>G. raimondii</i>, <i>G. arboreum</i>, and the two subgenomes each from <i>G. hir</i>s<i>utum</i> and <i>G. barbadense</i>) by using coding sequence alignment of orthologs from these genomes. Evolutionary rates (<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> and <i>K</i><sub>s</sub>) on each branch were estimated by using the Codeml program in the PAML package (version 4.9d)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 95" title="Yang, Z. PAML 4: phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Mol. Biol. Evol. 24, 1586â1591 (2007)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR95" id="ref-link-section-d10098e3099">95</a></sup>. We used the free-ratio âbranchâ model, which allows distinct evolutionary rates for each branch. The significance of rate variations between linages was examined through Wilcoxon rank-sum tests.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec28">Estimation of divergence time</h3><p>On the basis of the 21,419 cotton orthologous gene sets for <i>G. raimondii</i>, <i>G. arboreum</i>, and the two subgenomes each of <i>G. hirsutum</i> and <i>G. barbadense</i>, the synonymous divergence levels (<i>K</i><sub>s</sub>) for all four cotton species were calculated. The formula <i>t</i>â=â<i>K</i><sub>s</sub>/2<i>r</i> was used to estimate the divergence time between species, where <i>r</i> is the neutral substitution rate (<i>r</i>â=â2.6âÃâ10<sup>â9</sup>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec29">Phylogenetic tree of 13 D-genome diploids and 5 AD-genome allotetraploids</h3><p>Seventeen accessions comprising 13 D-genome diploid species and 3 wild allotetraploid species, <i>Gossypium tomentosum</i> (AD<sub>3</sub>), <i>Gossypium mustelinum</i> (AD<sub>4</sub>) and <i>Gossypium darwinii</i> (AD<sub>5</sub>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 96" title="Fryxell, P. A. The Natural History of the Cotton Tribe (Texas A&amp;M University Press, 1979)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR96" id="ref-link-section-d10098e3172">96</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 97" title="Grover, C. E., Kim, H., Wing, R. A., Paterson, A. H. &amp; Wendel, J. F. Incongruent patterns of local and global genome size evolution in cotton. Genome Res. 14, 1474â1482 (2004)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR97" id="ref-link-section-d10098e3175">97</a></sup>, were resequenced at high depth on the Illumina HiSeq 2500 platform, generating 85 Gb of sequencing data (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">16</a>). All reads were trimmed for quality with Sickle by using a minimum Phred quality threshold of 20 (<a href="https://github.com/najoshi/sickle">https://github.com/najoshi/sickle</a>). SNPs were then called by aligning the high-quality reads from the 17 accessions, Hai7124 and the 3 wild allotetraploid species against the TM-1 (v2.1) reference genome. The phylogenetic tree was constructed with the neighbor-joining method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 98" title="Saitou, N. &amp; Nei, M. The neighbor-joining method: a new method for reconstructing phylogenetic trees. Mol. Biol. Evol. 4, 406â425 (1987)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR98" id="ref-link-section-d10098e3189">98</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec30">Gene family expansion and contraction</h3><p>By using the gene families identified by the OrthoMCL (version 1.4) clustering program<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 94" title="Li, L., Stoeckert, C. J. Jr. &amp; Roos, D. S. OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res. 13, 2178â2189 (2003)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR94" id="ref-link-section-d10098e3201">94</a></sup>, we compared the number of genes clustered in each family between TM-1 and Hai7124. If the number of genes in TM-1 was more than twofold higher than that in Hai7124 and there were at least five genes in the gene family or the gene family was specific to TM-1, these gene families were defined as families that were expanded in TM-1 and as families that were contracted in Hai7124, and vice versa.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec31">Identification of SNPs, indels and structural variations between TM-1 and Hai7124</h3><p>The Hai7124 (v1.1) genome was aligned with the TM-1 (v2.1) genome by MUMmer (version 3.23; <a href="http://mummer.sourceforge.net/">http://mummer.sourceforge.net/</a>) with parameters â-maxmatch 90 -l 40â, and one-to-one genomic alignment results were extracted with the âdelta-filter -1â parameter. SNPs and indels were identified by show-snp from the one-to-one alignment blocks (parameter â-ClrTHâ). For identification of structural variations (PAVs, inversions and translocations), the alignment blocks from MUMmer with characteristics of structural variations were first extracted and the blocks with low similarity in the two flanking regions were filtered out.</p><p>To validate these variations, the following approaches were performed based on alignments of all available Illumina reads for TM-1 and Hai7124 to the TM-1 (v2.1) and Hai7124 (v1.1) genomes by BWA (version 0.7.15-r1140)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Fast and accurate short read alignment with BurrowsâWheeler transform. Bioinformatics 25, 1754â1760 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d10098e3223">62</a></sup>. In approach 1, at least four Illumina reads supporting the genotype were required to make us sufficiently confident of a candidate locus. In approach 2, Illumina reads were broken at the edge of the variation from the alignment results and variations with at least four supporting reads were defined as high-confidence variations. In approach 3, the single-end read/paired-end read ratio (S/P ratio) at the edge of the variation was computed and Fisherâs exact test was used to determine whether the variation was a supported candidate. SNPs were validated by approach 1. Indels (â¤100 bp) were validated by approach 1 or 2. Structural variations (&gt;100 bp; PAVs, inversions and translocations) were validated by approach 2 or 3. The putative functional effects of SNPs and indels were annotated with the ANNOVAR package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 99" title="Wang, K., Li, M. &amp; Hakonarson, H. ANNOVAR: functional annotation of genetic variants from high-throughput sequencing data. Nucleic Acids Res. 38, e164 (2010)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR99" id="ref-link-section-d10098e3227">99</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec32">Phylogenetic relationship and genomic variations between <i>G. hirsutum</i> and <i>G. barbadense</i>
                        </h3><p>Nineteen representative cotton accessions were subjected to deep resequencing on the Illumina HiSeq X Ten platform, producing 3.0 Tb of raw data, corresponding to a median of 60-fold coverage for each sample (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">17</a>). Paired-end resequencing reads from these accessions were mapped to the newly assembled TM-1 or Hai7124 genome by BWA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Fast and accurate short read alignment with BurrowsâWheeler transform. Bioinformatics 25, 1754â1760 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d10098e3249">62</a></sup> (version 0.7.15-r1140) with the MEM algorithm. After alignment, all mapped reads were sorted according to their aligned chromosomal position and potential PCR duplicates were removed with SAMtools (version 1.3.1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 100" title="Li, H. et al. The Sequence Alignment/Map format and SAMtools. Bioinformatics 25, 2078â2079 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR100" id="ref-link-section-d10098e3253">100</a></sup>. SNP calling was performed with the GATK (version 3.7-0-gcfedb67) UnifiedGenotyper<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 101" title="McKenna, A. et al. The Genome Analysis Toolkit: a MapReduce framework for analyzing next-generation DNA sequencing data. Genome Res. 20, 1297â1303 (2010)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR101" id="ref-link-section-d10098e3257">101</a></sup> and SAMtools (version 1.3.1) mpileup<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 100" title="Li, H. et al. The Sequence Alignment/Map format and SAMtools. Bioinformatics 25, 2078â2079 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR100" id="ref-link-section-d10098e3261">100</a></sup>. To obtain high-quality SNPs, only variations detected with both software packages with a sequencing depth of at least 10 and Phred-scaled quality score of at least 30 were retained for further analysis. The evolutionary history of these accessions was inferred by using the neighbor-joining tree<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 98" title="Saitou, N. &amp; Nei, M. The neighbor-joining method: a new method for reconstructing phylogenetic trees. Mol. Biol. Evol. 4, 406â425 (1987)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR98" id="ref-link-section-d10098e3266">98</a></sup>.</p><p>By using the indel and structural variation superset between TM-1 and Hai7124, the genotype of the 19 accessions was identified with the above validation approach with Illumina reads. Indels (â¤100 bp) were identified by approach 1 or 2. Structural variations (&gt;100 bp; PAVs, inversions and translocations) were identified by approach 2 or 3.</p><p>CNVs were identified by CNVKit<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 102" title="Talevich, E. et al. CNVkit: genome-wide copy number detection and visualization from targeted DNA sequencing. PLoS Comput. Biol. 12, e1004873 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#ref-CR102" id="ref-link-section-d10098e3276">102</a></sup> with gene annotations as targets. The segments identified with a log<sub>2</sub>-transformed copy number of at least 1.0 were considered to be copy number gain, and those with a log<sub>2</sub>-transformed copy number of â2.0 or less constituted to be copy number loss.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec33">Sampling and gene expression profiles under abiotic stresses</h3><p>Four-week-old seedlings were grouped in batches containing 15 seedlings each. Seedling batches were exposed to sodium chloride (0.4 M) and PEG (200 g/liter) as well as temperature regimes (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM1">47</a>). Leaves were collected and used for transcriptome sequencing. Differentially expressed genes were identified by using the DESeq2 package (<a href="http://www.bioconductor.org/packages/release/bioc/html/DESeq2.html">http://www.bioconductor.org/packages/release/bioc/html/DESeq2.html</a>), and raw <i>P</i> values from multiple tests were corrected by using FDR. In identifying differentially expressed genes, we required at least a twofold change in expression and a <i>P</i> value of less than 0.05.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec34">Quantitative RTâPCR analysis</h3><p>Gene-specific primers were designed on the basis of the candidate genes with WebSNAPER (<a href="http://pga.mgh.harvard.edu/cgi-bin/snap3/websnaper3.cgi">http://pga.mgh.harvard.edu/cgi-bin/snap3/websnaper3.cgi</a>). qRTâPCR was performed with SYBR Green Mastermix (Vazyme) on an ABI7500 instrument according to the manufacturerâs instructions. Gene expression was normalized against expression of the <i>GhEF1Î±</i> gene. Relative transcript levels were computed by using the 2<sup>âÎÎCt</sup> method on the basis of Ct values.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec35">Statistical analysis</h3><p>Comparison of codon substitution rate distributions between the two subgenomes of TM-1 and Hai7124 and their progenitors was carried out by using a two-sided Wilcoxon rank-sum test. Fisherâs exact test was employed to estimate whether a list of genes was enriched in a specific GO category when compared with background genes. <i>P</i> values were adjusted by FDR, and only GO terms with adjusted <i>P</i>â&lt;â0.05 were retained.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec36">Reporting Summary</h3><p>Further information on research design is available in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5#MOESM2">Nature Research Reporting Summary</a> linked to this article.</p></div></div></section>
                

                <section aria-labelledby="data-availability" data-title="Data availability"><div class="c-article-section" id="data-availability-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="data-availability">Data availability</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="data-availability-content">
              
              <p>The TM-1 (v2.1) and Hai7124 (v1.1) assembly and annotation data are available at <a href="http://ibi.zju.edu.cn/cotton">http://ibi.zju.edu.cn/cotton</a> and <a href="http://www.cottongen.org/">http://www.cottongen.org/</a>, respectively. The raw sequencing data used for de novo whole-genome assembly are available from the Sequence Read Archive under accession number <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/?term=PRJNA450479">PRJNA450479</a> and the China National GeneBank (CNGB) Nucleotide Sequence Archive (CNSA) under accession number <a href="https://db.cngb.org/cnsa/project/CNP0000046/public/">CNP0000046</a>. ChIP-seq data have been deposited in the Gene Expression Omnibus under accession number <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/geo/query/acc.cgi?acc=GSE119184">GSE119184</a>. All raw data for the 19 deep-sequenced accessions including 10 <i>G. hirsutum</i> and 9 <i>G. barbadense</i> accessions are available under project number <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/?term=PRJNA450479">PRJNA450479</a>, <a href="https://db.cngb.org/cnsa/project/CNP0000046/public/">CNP0000046</a> and <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/?term=PRJNA375965">PRJNA375965</a>. Illumina RNA-seq data are available at the Sequence Read Archive under accession number <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/?term=PRJNA490626">PRJNA490626</a>. Hi-C data and Bionano data for TM-1 and Hai7124 are available at the Sequence Read Archive under accession number <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/?term=PRJNA505106">PRJNA505106</a>. Further details on data accessibility are outlined in the supplementary materials and methods.</p>

Zhang and Y.H. conceptualized the project. Y.H., J.C., L.F., Z.Z., W.M. and T. Zhang planned and coordinated the project. Z.H., H.W., C.X., W.S. and D.Y. collected and grew the plant materials and prepared DNA and RNA for Illumina sequencing and PacBio SMRT sequencing. J.D., Z.H., Z.S., Y. Zang, X.Z. and B.Z. developed the F<sub>2</sub> populations and prepared DNA for sequencing. K.B., A.B. and H.B. performed genome assembly. Y.N., J.L., L.Z. and F.H. conducted genome annotation and synteny analyses. J.C. constructed the genetic map and performed the structural variation study. Z.Z. and W.M. analyzed the genes related to fiber development and abiotic stresses. J.H. and K.W. identified centromeric regions by ChIP-seq. Q.W., L.J., T. Zhao, G.M., X.G., F.D., Y.B., X.L. and Y. Zhang were involved in data analysis. L.F., Y.N. and J.C. carried out data submission. Y.H., L.F., Z.Z., W.M., T. Zhang, M.R., Y.R., X.C., D.F. and S.Z. wrote the manuscript. All authors read and approved the manuscript.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="corresponding-author">Corresponding author</h3><p id="corresponding-author-list">Correspondence to
                <a id="corresp-c1" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41588-019-0371-5/email/correspondent/c1/new">Tianzhen Zhang</a>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="ethics" data-title="Ethics declarations"><div class="c-article-section" id="ethics-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="ethics">Ethics declarations</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="ethics-content">
